<div

col-a-2">













<div
class="header
artist_info_header
int-1
clearfix">














<h1
class="hed-1"
title="Eric
Clapton"
itemprop="name">Eric
Clapton</h1>














<div
class="header_btns">















<div
class="bookmark"
data-musicid="R59097"></div>














</div>













</div>













<div
class="int-1">














<div
class="content_right">















<div
class="artist_bio_container
clearfix">
















<div
class="artist_bio
short_bio">
















<div
class="artist_bio_inner">

















By
the
time
Eric
Clapton
launched
his
solo
career
with
the
release
of
his
self-titled
debut
album
in
mid-1970,
he
was
long
established
as
one
of
the
world's
major
rock
stars
due
to
his
group
affiliations
--


















<a
href="/yardbirds"
class="hash">the
Yardbirds</a>,
John
Mayall's
Bluesbreakers,


















<a
href="/cream"
class="hash">Cream</a>,
and


















<a
href="/blind-faith"
class="hash">Blind
Faith</a>
--
which
had
demonstrated
his
claim
to
being
the
best
rock
guitarist
of
his
generation.
That
it
took
Clapton
so
long
to
go
out
on
his
own,
however,
was
evidence
of
a
degree
of
reticence
unusual
for
one
of
his
stature.
And
his
debut
album,
though
it
spawned
the
Top
40
hit
&quot;After
Midnight,&quot;
was
typical
of
his
self-effacing
approach:
it
was,
in
effect,
an
album
by
the
group
he
had
lately
been
featured
in,
Delaney
&amp;
Bonnie
&amp;
Friends.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Not
surprisingly,
before
his
solo
debut
had
even
been
released,
Clapton
had
retreated
from
his
solo
stance,
assembling
from
the
D&amp;B&amp;F
ranks
the
personnel
for
a
group,


















<a
href="/derek-dominos"
class="hash">Derek
&amp;
the
Dominos</a>,
with
whom
he
played
for
most
of
1970
and
recorded
the
landmark
album


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">Layla
and
Other
Assorted
Love
Songs</a>.
Clapton
was
largely
inactive
in
1971
and
1972,
due
to
heroin
addiction,
but
he
performed
a
comeback
concert
at
the
Rainbow
Theatre
in
London
on
January
13,
1973,
resulting
in
the
album


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">Eric
Clapton's
Rainbow
Concert</a>
(September
1973).
But
Clapton
did
not
launch
a
sustained
solo
career
until
July
1974,
when
he
released


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/461-ocean-boulevard"
class="hash">461
Ocean
Boulevard</a>,
which
topped
the
charts
and
spawned
the
number
one
single
&quot;I
Shot
the
Sheriff.&quot;


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
The
persona
Clapton
established
over
the
next
decade
was
less
that
of
guitar
hero
than
arena
rock
star
with
a
weakness
for
ballads.
The
follow-ups
to


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/461-ocean-boulevard"
class="hash">461
Ocean
Boulevard</a>,
There's
One
in
Every
Crowd
(March
1975),
the
live


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">E.C.
Was
Here</a>
(August
1975),
and


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/no-reason-to-cry"
class="hash">No
Reason
to
Cry</a>
(August
1976),
were
less
successful.
But


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/slowhand"
class="hash">Slowhand</a>
(November
1977),
which
featured
both
the
powerful
&quot;Cocaine&quot;
(written
by


















<a
href="/jj-cale-eric-clapton"
class="hash">J.J.
Cale</a>,
who
had
also
written
&quot;After
Midnight&quot;)
and
the
hit
singles
&quot;Lay
Down
Sally&quot;
and
&quot;Wonderful
Tonight,&quot;
was
a
million-seller.
Its
follow-ups,


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/backless"
class="hash">Backless</a>
(November
1978),
featuring
the
Top
Ten
hit
&quot;Promises,&quot;
the
live


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">Just
One
Night</a>
(April
1980),
and


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/another-ticket"
class="hash">Another
Ticket</a>
(February
1981),
featuring
the
Top
Ten
hit
&quot;I
Can't
Stand
It,&quot;
were
all
big
sellers.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Clapton's
popularity
waned
somewhat
in
the
first
half
of
the
'80s,
as
the
albums


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/money-cigarettes"
class="hash">Money
and
Cigarettes</a>
(February
1983),


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/behind-sun"
class="hash">Behind
the
Sun</a>
(March
1985),
and


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">August</a>
(November
1986)
indicated
a
certain
career
stasis.
But
he
was
buoyed
up
by
the
release
of
the
box
set
retrospective


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">Crossroads</a>
(April
1988),
which
seemed
to
remind
his
fans
of
how
great
he
was.


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/journeyman"
class="hash">Journeyman</a>
(November
1989)
was
a
return
to
form.
It
would
be
his
last
new
studio
album
for
nearly
five
years,
though
in
the
interim
he
would
suffer
greatly
and
enjoy
surprising
triumph.
On
March
20,
1991,
Clapton's
four-year-old
son
was
killed
in
a
fall.
While
he
mourned,
he
released
a
live
album,


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">24
Nights</a>
(October
1991),
culled
from
his
annual
concert
series
at
Royal
Albert
Hall
in
London,
and
prepared
a
movie
soundtrack,


















<a
href="/rush-film-score/rush-music-from-motion-picture-soundtrack"
class="hash">Rush</a>
(January
1992).
The
soundtrack
featured
a
song
written
for
his
son,
&quot;Tears
in
Heaven,&quot;
that
became
a
massive
hit
single.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
In
March
1992,
Clapton
recorded
a
concert
for


















<i>MTV
Unplugged</i>
which,
when
released
on
an
album
in
August,
became
his
biggest-selling
record
ever.
Two
years
later,
Clapton
returned
with
a
blues
album,


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/from-cradle"
class="hash">From
the
Cradle</a>,
which
became
one
of
his
most
successful
albums,
both
commercially
and
critically.
Crossroads,
Vol.
2:
Live
in
the
Seventies,
a
box
set
chronicling
his
live
work
from
the
'70s,
was
released
to
mixed
reviews.
In
early
1997,
Clapton,
billing
himself
by
the
pseudonym
&quot;X-Sample,&quot;
collaborated
with
keyboardist/producer
Simon
Climie
as
the
ambient
new
age
and
trip-hop
duo
T.D.F.
The
duo
released
Retail
Therapy
to
mixed
reviews
in
early
1997.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Clapton
retained
Climie
as
his
collaborator
for


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/pilgrim"
class="hash">Pilgrim</a>,
his
first
album
of
new
material
since
1989's


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/journeyman"
class="hash">Journeyman</a>.


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/pilgrim"
class="hash">Pilgrim</a>
was
greeted
with
decidedly
mixed
reviews
upon
its
spring
1998
release,
but
the
album
debuted
at
number
four
and
stayed
in
the
Top
Ten
for
several
weeks
on
the
success
of
the
single
&quot;My
Father's
Eyes.&quot;
In
2000,
Clapton
teamed
up
with
old
friend


















<a
href="/bb-king-eric-clapton"
class="hash">B.B.
King</a>
on


















<a
href="/bb-king-eric-clapton/riding-with-king"
class="hash">Riding
with
the
King</a>,
a
set
of
blues
standards
and
material
from
contemporary
singer/songwriters.
Another
solo
outing,
entitled


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/reptile"
class="hash">Reptile</a>,
followed
in
early
2001.
Three
years
later,
Clapton
issued


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/me-mr-johnson"
class="hash">Me
and
Mr.
Johnson</a>,
a
collection
of
tunes
honoring
the
Mississippi-born
bluesman


















<a
href="/robert-johnson"
class="hash">Robert
Johnson</a>.
Released
in
2005,


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/back-home"
class="hash">Back
Home</a>,
Clapton's
14th
album
of
original
material,
reflected
his
ease
with
fatherhood.
Also
in
2005,
Clapton
unexpectedly
teamed
with


















<a
href="/jack-bruce"
class="hash">Jack
Bruce</a>
and


















<a
href="/ginger-bakers-airforce"
class="hash">Ginger
Baker</a>
for
a


















<a
href="/cream"
class="hash">Cream</a>
reunion
that
included
May
concerts
at
Londons
Royal
Albert
Hall
and
shows
at
New
Yorks
Madison
Square
Garden
in
October,
with
the
former
being
compiled
for
a
live
release
that
fall.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
This
turned
out
to
be
the
first
of
many
reunions
and
looks
back
for
Clapton.
In
2006,
he
elevated
the
profile
of
his
latter-day
idol


















<a
href="/jj-cale-eric-clapton"
class="hash">J.J.
Cale</a>
by
recording
an
album-long
duet,


















<a
href="/jj-cale-eric-clapton/road-to-escondido"
class="hash">The
Road
to
Escondido</a>.
The
following
year
he
released
his
autobiography
--
accompanied
by
a
new
career
compilation
called
The
Complete
Clapton
--
which
focused
more
on
his
trials
with
addiction
and
subsequent
recovery
than
his
musical
career.
In
2008,
Clapton
began
playing
regular
shows
with
his
old


















<a
href="/blind-faith"
class="hash">Blind
Faith</a>
partner


















<a
href="/steve-winwood"
class="hash">Steve
Winwood</a>,
gigs
that
were
captured
on
the
2009
double-live
set


















<a
href="/eric-clapton-steve-winwood/live-from-madison-square-garden"
class="hash">Live
from
Madison
Square
Garden</a>.


















<a
href="/steve-winwood"
class="hash">Winwood</a>
also
appeared
on
Claptons
next
studio
album,
2010s


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">Clapton</a>,
which
was
a
collaboration-heavy
affair
also
featuring


















<a
href="/jj-cale-eric-clapton"
class="hash">Cale</a>,


















<a
href="/sheryl-crow"
class="hash">Sheryl
Crow</a>,


















<a
href="/allen-toussaint"
class="hash">Allen
Toussaint</a>,
and


















<a
href="/wynton-marsalis-classical"
class="hash">Wynton
Marsalis</a>.
In
2011,
Clapton
returned
the
favor
to


















<a
href="/wynton-marsalis-classical"
class="hash">Marsalis</a>
by
collaborating
on
the
live
concert
album
Play
the
Blues:
Live
from
Jazz
at
Lincoln
Center.


















<br
/>

















<br
/>
Clapton
parted
ways
with
Warner
after


















<a
href="#"
class="hash">Clapton</a>,
and
he
chose
to
set
up
his
own
Bushbranch
imprint
on


















<a
href="/gary-hoey"
class="hash">Gary
Hoey</a>'s
independent
label
Surfdog.
His
first
album
for
the
label
was


















<a
href="/eric-clapton/old-sock"
class="hash">Old
Sock</a>,
largely
a
collection
of
old
songs
the
guitarist
loved.
~
William
Ruhlmann,
Rovi
















</div>















</div>
















<a
href="#"
class="full_bio_link">full
bio</a>















</div>

<div>






















<a
class="track_link"
itemprop="url"
title="I
Can't
Hold
Out"
href="/eric-clapton/give-me-strength-74-75-studio-recordings/i-cant-hold-out">6.
<span
itemprop="name">I
Can't

I
could
not
listen
to
this
song
for
several
years
because
it
felt
as
though
it
would
jinx
my
loved
ones.
I
listened
to
it
twice
in
one
week
on
the
radio
and
a
few
weeks
later
my
beloved
nephew
died.
But
now
I'm
at
peace
when
I
hear
this
and
it
does
not
bother
me.
